					<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading mw-first-heading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Adult neurogenesis</span></h1>
							
<div id="p-lang-btn" class="vector-dropdown mw-portlet mw-portlet-lang"  >
	<input type="checkbox" id="p-lang-btn-checkbox" role="button" aria-haspopup="true" data-event-name="ui.dropdown-p-lang-btn" class="vector-dropdown-checkbox mw-interlanguage-selector" aria-label="Go to an article in another language. Available in 4 languages"   >
	<label id="p-lang-btn-label" for="p-lang-btn-checkbox" class="vector-dropdown-label cdx-button cdx-button--fake-button cdx-button--fake-button--enabled cdx-button--weight-quiet cdx-button--action-progressive mw-portlet-lang-heading-4" aria-hidden="true"  ><span class="vector-icon mw-ui-icon-language-progressive mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-language-progressive"></span>

<span class="vector-dropdown-label-text">4 languages</span>
	</label>
	<div class="vector-dropdown-content">

		<div class="vector-menu-content">
			
			<ul class="vector-menu-content-list">
				
				<li class="interlanguage-link interwiki-ar mw-list-item"><a href="https://ar.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D8%AA%D8%AE%D9%84%D9%82_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%86%D8%B3%D9%8A%D8%AC_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%B9%D8%B5%D8%A8%D9%8A_%D8%B9%D9%86%D8%AF_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%A8%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%BA%D9%8A%D9%86" title="تخلق النسيج العصبي عند البالغين – Arabic" lang="ar" hreflang="ar" data-title="تخلق النسيج العصبي عند البالغين" data-language-autonym="العربية" data-language-local-name="Arabic" class="interlanguage-link-target"><span>العربية</span></a></li><li class="interlanguage-link interwiki-es mw-list-item"><a href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neurog%C3%A9nesis_adulta" title="Neurogénesis adulta – Spanish" lang="es" hreflang="es" data-title="Neurogénesis adulta" data-language-autonym="Español" data-language-local-name="Spanish" class="interlanguage-link-target"><span>Español</span></a></li><li class="interlanguage-link interwiki-ru badge-Q70893996 mw-list-item" title=""><a href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%9D%D0%B5%D0%B9%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B7_%D1%83_%D0%B2%D0%B7%D1%80%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BB%D1%8B%D1%85" title="Нейрогенез у взрослых – Russian" lang="ru" hreflang="ru" data-title="Нейрогенез у взрослых" data-language-autonym="Русский" data-language-local-name="Russian" class="interlanguage-link-target"><span>Русский</span></a></li><li class="interlanguage-link interwiki-uk badge-Q70893996 mw-list-item" title=""><a href="https://uk.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%9D%D0%B5%D0%B9%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B7_%D0%B2_%D0%B4%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%B8%D1%85" title="Нейрогенез в дорослих – Ukrainian" lang="uk" hreflang="uk" data-title="Нейрогенез в дорослих" data-language-autonym="Українська" data-language-local-name="Ukrainian" class="interlanguage-link-target"><span>Українська</span></a></li>
			</ul>
			<div class="after-portlet after-portlet-lang"><span class="wb-langlinks-edit wb-langlinks-link"><a href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Special:EntityPage/Q23807835#sitelinks-wikipedia" title="Edit interlanguage links" class="wbc-editpage">Edit links</a></span></div>

				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>
					<div class="vector-body-before-content">
							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Generating of neurons from neural stem cells in adults</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Neuroregeneration" title="Neuroregeneration">Neuroregeneration</a>.</div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Proliferating_cells_in_the_dentate_gyrus_(crop).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Proliferating_cells_in_the_dentate_gyrus_%28crop%29.jpg/250px-Proliferating_cells_in_the_dentate_gyrus_%28crop%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="386" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Proliferating_cells_in_the_dentate_gyrus_%28crop%29.jpg/500px-Proliferating_cells_in_the_dentate_gyrus_%28crop%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="595" data-file-height="956" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Bromodeoxyuridine" title="Bromodeoxyuridine">BrdU</a> (red), a marker of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> replication, highlights neurogenesis in the <a href="/wiki/Subgranular_zone" title="Subgranular zone">subgranular zone</a> of hippocampal <a href="/wiki/Dentate_gyrus" title="Dentate gyrus">dentate gyrus</a>. Fragment of an <a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Phenotypes_of_proliferating_cells_in_the_Rostral_Migratory_Stream_and_Dentate_Gyrus.jpg" class="extiw" title="commons:File:Phenotypes of proliferating cells in the Rostral Migratory Stream and Dentate Gyrus.jpg">illustration</a> from Faiz et al., 2005.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid15826306&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15826306-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Doublecortin_expression.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Doublecortin_expression.png/250px-Doublecortin_expression.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="386" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Doublecortin_expression.png/500px-Doublecortin_expression.png 1.5x" data-file-width="992" data-file-height="1530" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Doublecortin" title="Doublecortin">Doublecortin</a> expression in the rat <a href="/wiki/Dentate_gyrus" title="Dentate gyrus">dentate gyrus</a>, 21st postnatal day. Oomen et al., 2009.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dcx&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dcx-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p><b>Adult neurogenesis</b> is the process in which <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> are generated from <a href="/wiki/Neural_stem_cell" title="Neural stem cell">neural stem cells</a> in the adult. This process differs from <a href="/wiki/Prenatal_development" title="Prenatal development">prenatal</a> <a href="/wiki/Neurogenesis" title="Neurogenesis">neurogenesis</a>.
</p><p>In most mammals, new neurons are born throughout adulthood in two regions of the brain:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid25621867&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25621867-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Subgranular_zone" title="Subgranular zone">subgranular zone</a> (SGZ), part of the <a href="/wiki/Dentate_gyrus" title="Dentate gyrus">dentate gyrus</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">hippocampus</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dentate&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dentate-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> where neural stem cells give birth to <a href="/wiki/Granule_cell" title="Granule cell">granule cells</a> (implicated in memory formation and learning).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Subventricular_zone" title="Subventricular zone">subventricular zone</a> (SVZ) of the <a href="/wiki/Lateral_ventricle" class="mw-redirect" title="Lateral ventricle">lateral ventricles</a>, which can be divided into three microdomains: lateral, dorsal and medial.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Neural stem cells migrate to the <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_bulb" title="Olfactory bulb">olfactory bulb</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Rostral_migratory_stream" title="Rostral migratory stream">rostral migratory stream</a> where they differentiate into <a href="/wiki/Interneuron" title="Interneuron">interneurons</a> participating in the <a href="/wiki/Olfaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Olfaction">sense of smell</a>.  In humans, however, few if any olfactory bulb neurons are generated after birth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid24561062&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24561062-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<p>More attention has been given to the neurogenesis in the <a href="/wiki/Dentate_gyrus" title="Dentate gyrus">dentate gyrus</a> than in the <a href="/wiki/Striatum" title="Striatum">striatum</a>.  In <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a>, many of the newborn dentate gyrus neurons die shortly after they are born,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dentate&#95;4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dentate-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but a number of them become functionally integrated into the surrounding brain tissue.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Adult neurogenesis in rodents is reported to play a role in learning and memory, emotion, stress, depression, response to injury, and other conditions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The numbers of neurons born in the human adult hippocampus remains controversial; some studies have reported that in adult humans about 700 new neurons are added in the hippocampus every day,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while more recent studies show that adult hippocampal neurogenesis does not exist in humans, or, if it does, it is at undetectable levels.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Recent evidence shows that adult neurogenesis is essentially extinct in humans.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The experiments advocating for the presence of adult neurogenesis have focused on how dual antigen retrieval finds that DCX antibodies are staining many cells within the adult human dentate gyrus. This finding is not as clear though as supporters of adult neurogenesis suggest; the dentate gyrus cells stained with DCX have been shown to have a mature morphology, contrasting the idea that novel neurons are being generated within the adult brain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The role of new neurons in human adult brain function thus remains unclear.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Mechanism">Mechanism</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Mechanism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Adult_neural_stem_cells">Adult neural stem cells</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Adult neural stem cells"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Adult_stem_cell" title="Adult stem cell">Adult stem cell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neural_stem_cell" title="Neural stem cell">Neural stem cell</a></div>
<p>Neural <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">stem cells</a> (NSCs) are the self-renewing, <a href="/wiki/Multipotency" class="mw-redirect" title="Multipotency">multipotent</a> cells that generate the main <a href="/wiki/Phenotypes" class="mw-redirect" title="Phenotypes">phenotypes</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Lineage_reprogramming_(trans-differentiation)"><span id="Lineage_reprogramming_.28trans-differentiation.29"></span>Lineage reprogramming (trans-differentiation)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Lineage reprogramming (trans-differentiation)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Emerging evidence suggests that neural microvascular pericytes, under instruction from resident glial cells, are reprogrammed into interneurons and enrich local neuronal microcircuits.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This response is amplified by concomitant <a href="/wiki/Angiogenesis" title="Angiogenesis">angiogenesis</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Model_organisms_of_neurogenesis">Model organisms of neurogenesis</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Model organisms of neurogenesis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">Model organism</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Planarian">Planarian</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Planarian"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Planarian" title="Planarian">Planarian</a> are one of the earliest <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organisms</a> used to study <a href="/wiki/Regeneration_(biology)" title="Regeneration (biology)">regeneration</a> with <a href="/wiki/Peter_Simon_Pallas" title="Peter Simon Pallas">Pallas</a> as the forefather of planarian studies. Planarian are a classical invertebrate model that in recent decades have been used to examine neurogenesis. The central nervous system of a planarian is simple, though fully formed with two lobes located in the head and two <a href="/wiki/Ventral_nerve_cord" title="Ventral nerve cord">ventral nerve cords</a>. This model reproduces asexually producing a complete and fully functioning nervous system after division allowing for consistent examination of neurogenesis.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Axolotl">Axolotl</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Axolotl"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Axolotl" title="Axolotl">axolotl</a> is less commonly used than other vertebrates, but is still a classical model for examining regeneration and neurogenesis. Though the axolotl has made its place in biomedical research in terms of limb regeneration,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the model organism has displayed a robust ability to generate new neurons following damage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Axolotls have contributed as a bridge organism between invertebrates and mammals, as the species has the regenerative capacity to undergo complete neurogenesis forming a wide range of neuronal populations not limited to a small niche,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> yet the complexity and architecture is complex and analogous in many ways to human neural development.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Zebrafish">Zebrafish</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Zebrafish"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Zebrafish" title="Zebrafish">Zebrafish</a> have long been a classical <a href="/wiki/Human_development_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Human development (biology)">developmental</a> model due to their transparency during <a href="/wiki/Organogenesis" title="Organogenesis">organogenesis</a> and have been utilized heavily in early development neurogenesis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The zebrafish displays a strong neurogenerative capacity capable of regenerating a variety of tissues and complete neuronal diversity (with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Astrocyte" title="Astrocyte">astrocytes</a>, as they have yet to be identified within the zebrafish brain) with continued neurogenesis through the life span. In recent decades the model has solidified its role in adult regeneration and neurogenesis following damage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The zebrafish, like the axolotl, has played a key role as a bridge organism between invertebrates and mammals. The zebrafish is a rapidly developing organism that is relatively inexpensive to maintain, while providing the field ease of genetic manipulation and a complex nervous system.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Chick">Chick</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Chick"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Though avians have been used primarily to study early embryonic development, in recent decades the developing <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chick</a> has played a critical role in the examination of neurogenesis and regeneration as the young chick is capable of neuronal-turnover at a young age, but loses the neurogenerative capacity into adulthood.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The loss of neuroregenerative ability over maturation has allowed investigators to further examine genetic regulators of neurogenesis.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Rodents">Rodents</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Rodents"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Animal_testing_on_rodents" title="Animal testing on rodents">Animal testing on rodents</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">Rodents</a>, mice and rats, have been the most prominent model organism since the discovery of modern neurons by <a href="/wiki/Santiago_Ram%C3%B3n_y_Cajal" title="Santiago Ramón y Cajal">Santiago Ramon y Cajal</a>. Rodents have a very similar architecture and a complex nervous system with very little regenerative capacity similar to that found in humans. For that reason, rodents have been heavily used in <a href="/wiki/Pre-clinical_development" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-clinical development">pre-clinical testing</a>. Rodents display a wide range of neural circuits responsible for complex behaviors making them ideal for studies of dendritic pruning and axonal shearing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While the organism makes for a strong human analog, the model has its limitations not found in the previous models: higher cost of maintenance, lower breeding numbers, and the limited neurogenerative abilities.
</p><p>To some extent, adult neurogenesis in rodents may be induced by selective disruption of <a href="/wiki/Notch_signalling" class="mw-redirect" title="Notch signalling">Notch signalling</a> in <a href="/wiki/Astrocytes" class="mw-redirect" title="Astrocytes">astrocytes</a>:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> this produces novel neurons which functionally integrate into the <a href="/wiki/Striatal" class="mw-redirect" title="Striatal">striatal</a> circuit.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Adult neurogenesis in the subventricular zone and dentate gyrus of rodents generates <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_stress" title="Oxidative stress">oxidative stress</a> and production of <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a> that can damage both <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Walton2012&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walton2012-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The oxidative stress caused by postnatal neurogenesis may significantly contribute to the reduced <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a> that occurs with increasing age.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Walton2012&#95;34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walton2012-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Octopus">Octopus</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Octopus"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopod</a> also known as the <a href="/wiki/Common_octopus" title="Common octopus">common octopus</a>, this organism has an intricate nervous system that demonstrates the brain's capacity to produce new cells. In this case and in other taxa when compared,  these organisms adapt to unpredictable environments by using newly formed brain cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is over a short life-span (female about one year) where wild common octopuses focus most of their energy on mating and offspring care.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid25449183&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25449183-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Findings suggest that the octopus vulgaris like other short-lived species have a complex hippocampal proliferation,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid18957357&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18957357-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid21929629&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21929629-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> needed for spatial/navigation, and short and long-term memory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Wikipedia_and_sources_that_mirror_or_use_it" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="This claim cites another Wikipedia article. Articles need references to reliable third-party sources. (May 2020)">circular reference</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Chickadees">Chickadees</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Chickadees"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Adult_neurogenesis_in_songbirds" title="Adult neurogenesis in songbirds">Adult neurogenesis in songbirds</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Black-capped_chickadees" class="mw-redirect" title="Black-capped chickadees">Black-capped chickadees</a> are a well-known <a href="/wiki/Model_species" class="mw-redirect" title="Model species">model species</a> in the field of neuroscience for their neural mechanisms in <a href="/wiki/Bird_vocalization" title="Bird vocalization">song vocalization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Synaptic_plasticity" title="Synaptic plasticity">plasticity</a>, and memory. Black-capped chickadees are different from other species in the larger group of songbirds because they are characterized by food-caching behaviors. Due to this behavior, chickadees can be described through their remarkable <a href="/wiki/Spatial_memory" title="Spatial memory">spatial memory</a>. Seasonal changes in hippocampal densities have been described since 1994<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> where neuronal survival peaks during the fall (October),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> measured by <a href="/wiki/Thymidine" title="Thymidine">thymidine</a> (see tracking neurogenesis below) labeled cells, weeks after injection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When compared to non-food caching birds such as the house sparrow, chickadees had significantly more hippocampal neuron recruitment from fall to spring.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The changes in hippocampal density is directly associated with increased hoarding behavior,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> especially during the winter when better spatial memory maximizes their survival.
</p><p>Over the 2 decades since the initial discovery,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the specific role of chickadee hippocampus in memory has gained wide attention. In an experimental setting, hippocampal lesions affect memory for locations,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> validating previous notions for this specific role. Further, experimentally inhibiting neuronal proliferation decreases scores on spatial memory tasks,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> supporting that new neurons hold the same role as pre-existing ones. The specific function of the hippocampus, coupled with seasonal changes in their volume, point towards their temporary advantages for spatial memory consolidation. Taken all together, adult neurogenesis in the hippocampus of black-capped chickadees suggest a selective mechanisms for neuronal survival in direct correlation with seasonal food caching behavior.
</p><p>Developmentally, <a href="/wiki/Progenitor_cell" title="Progenitor cell">progenitor cells</a> called <a href="/wiki/Radial_glial_cell" title="Radial glial cell">radial glial</a> cells are thought to mitigate newly born neurons to their destinations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Radial glial cells extend processes from their soma in the avian ventricular zone to the parenchyma of the adult forebrain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These new neurons have been observed as early as 3 days after thymidine administration in the HVC<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and as early as 7 days before reaching the hippocampus.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Avian migration of new neurons are analogous to mammalian species,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> providing a future direction in exploring neurogenesis in mammalian species and beyond. However, captivity has been shown to reduce hippocampal volumes when compared to wild counterparts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Reduced neurogenesis in captive birds may be caused by stress, lack of exercise, diminished social interaction, and limited caching opportunities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Tracking_neurogenesis">Tracking neurogenesis</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Tracking neurogenesis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The creation of new functional <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> can be measured in several ways,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> summarized in the following sections.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="DNA_labelling">DNA labelling</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: DNA labelling"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Labelled <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> can trace dividing cell's lineage, and determine the location of its daughter cells. A <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acid</a> analog is inserted into the <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> of a neuron-generating cell (such as a <a href="/wiki/Neuroglia" class="mw-redirect" title="Neuroglia">glial cell</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neural_stem_cell" title="Neural stem cell">neural stem cell</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ming,&#95;Guo-Li&#95;2011&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ming,_Guo-Li_2011-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thymine" title="Thymine">Thymine</a> analogs (3H) <a href="/wiki/Thymidine" title="Thymidine">thymidine</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Altman62&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altman62-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bromodeoxyuridine" title="Bromodeoxyuridine">BrdU</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> are commonly used DNA labels, and are used for <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_tracer" title="Radioactive tracer">radiolabelling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Immunohistochemistry" title="Immunohistochemistry">immunohistochemistry</a> respectively.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Fate_determination_via_neuronal_lineage_markers">Fate determination via neuronal lineage markers</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Fate determination via neuronal lineage markers"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> labeling can be used in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Neuronal_lineage_marker" title="Neuronal lineage marker">neuronal lineage markers</a> to determine the <a href="/wiki/Cell_fate_determination" title="Cell fate determination">fate</a> of new functional <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">brain cells</a>. First, incorporated labeled <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a> are used to detect the populations of newly <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">divided daughter cells</a>. Specific cell types are then determined with unique differences in their <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_gene_expression" title="Regulation of gene expression">expression</a> of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, which can be used as <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigens</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Immunoassay" title="Immunoassay">immunoassay</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/NeuN" title="NeuN">NeuN/Fox3</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glial_fibrillary_acidic_protein" title="Glial fibrillary acidic protein">GFAP</a> are <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigens</a> commonly used to detect <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neuroglia" class="mw-redirect" title="Neuroglia">glia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ependyma" title="Ependyma">ependymal cells</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ki-67_(protein)" title="Ki-67 (protein)">Ki67</a> is the most commonly used antigen to detect <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">cell proliferation</a>. 
Some <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigens</a> can be used to measure specific <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">stem cell</a> stages. For example, stem cells requires the <a href="/wiki/Sox2" class="mw-redirect" title="Sox2">sox2</a> gene to maintain <a href="/wiki/Pluripotency" class="mw-redirect" title="Pluripotency">pluripotency</a> and is used to detect enduring concentrations of stem cells in <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">CNS tissue</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> <a href="/wiki/Nestin_(protein)" title="Nestin (protein)">nestin</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_filaments" class="mw-redirect" title="Intermediate filaments">intermediate filament</a>, which is essential for the radial growth of <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">axons</a>, and is therefore used to detect the formation of new <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapses</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cre-Lox_recombination">Cre-Lox recombination</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cre-Lox recombination"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Some genetic tracing studies utilize <a href="/wiki/Cre-Lox_recombination" title="Cre-Lox recombination">cre-lox recombination</a> to bind a <a href="/wiki/Promoter_(genetics)" title="Promoter (genetics)">promoter</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Reporter_gene" title="Reporter gene">reporter gene</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Lac_operon" title="Lac operon">lacZ</a> or <a href="/wiki/Green_fluorescent_protein" title="Green fluorescent protein">GFP gene</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ming,&#95;Guo-Li&#95;2011&#95;50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ming,_Guo-Li_2011-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This method can be used for long term quantification of cell division and labeling, whereas the previously mentioned procedures are only useful for short-term quantification.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Viral_vectors">Viral vectors</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Viral vectors"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>It has recently become more common to use <a href="/wiki/Recombinant_DNA" title="Recombinant DNA">recombinant</a> viruses to insert the <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_sequence" title="Nucleic acid sequence">genetic information</a> encoding specific <a href="/wiki/Biomarker" title="Biomarker">markers</a> (usually protein <a href="/wiki/Fluorophore" title="Fluorophore">fluorophores</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Green_fluorescent_protein" title="Green fluorescent protein">GFP</a>) that are only <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">expressed</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> of a certain kind. The marker gene is inserted <a href="/wiki/Downstream_promoter_element" title="Downstream promoter element">downstream</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Promoter_(genetics)" title="Promoter (genetics)">promoter</a>, leading to <a href="/wiki/Transcription_(biology)" title="Transcription (biology)">transcription</a> of that marker only in cells containing the <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factor(s)</a> that bind to that promoter. For example, a recombinant <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmid</a> may contain the promoter for <a href="/wiki/Doublecortin" title="Doublecortin">doublecortin</a>, a protein expressed predominantly by <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>, upstream of a sequence coding for <a href="/wiki/Green_fluorescent_protein" title="Green fluorescent protein">GFP</a>, thereby making infected cells <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">fluoresce</a> green upon exposure to light in the blue to ultraviolet range<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while leaving non <a href="/wiki/Doublecortin" title="Doublecortin">doublecortin</a> expressing cells unaffected, even if they contain the <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmid</a>. Many cells will contain multiple copies of the plasmid and the fluorophore itself, allowing the fluorescent properties to be transferred along an infected cell's <a href="/wiki/Cell_lineage" title="Cell lineage">lineage</a>.
</p><p>By labeling a cell that gives rise to neurons, such as a <a href="/wiki/Neural_stem_cell" title="Neural stem cell">neural stem cells</a> or neural precursor cells, one can track the creation, proliferation, and even migration of newly created neurons.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is important to note, however, that while the plasmid is stable for long periods of time, its protein products may have highly variable <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half lives</a> and their fluorescence may decrease as well as become too diluted to be seen depending on the number of round of replication they have undergone, making this method more useful for tracking self-similar neural precursor or neural stem cells rather than neurons themselves. The insertion of <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genetic material</a> via a <a href="/wiki/Viral_vector" title="Viral vector">viral vector</a> tends to be sporadic and infrequent relative to the total number of cells in a given region of <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissue</a>, making quantification of cell division inaccurate. However, the above method can provide highly accurate data with respect to when a cell was <a href="/wiki/Cell_growth" title="Cell growth">born</a> as well as full cellular <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphologies</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Methods_for_inhibiting_neurogenesis">Methods for inhibiting neurogenesis</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Methods for inhibiting neurogenesis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Many studies analyzing the role of adult neurogenesis utilize a method of inhibiting cell proliferation in specific brain regions, mimicking an inhibition of neurogenesis, to observe the effects on behavior.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Pharmacological_inhibition">Pharmacological inhibition</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Pharmacological inhibition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Pharmacological inhibition is widely used in various studies, as it provides many benefits. It is generally inexpensive as compared to other methods, such as irradiation, can be used on various species, and does not require any invasive procedures or surgeries for the subjects.
</p><p>However, it does pose certain challenges, as these inhibitors can't be used to inhibit proliferation in specific regions, thus leading to nonspecific effects from other systems being affected. To avoid these effects, more work must be done to determine optimal doses in order to minimize the effects on systems unrelated to neurogenesis.
</p><p>A common pharmacological inhibitor for adult neurogenesis is methylazoxymethanol acetate (MAM), a chemotherapeutic agent. Other cell division inhibitors commonly used in studies are cytarabine and temozolomide.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Pharmacogenetics">Pharmacogenetics</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Pharmacogenetics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Another method used to study the effects of adult neurogenesis is using pharmacogenetic models. These models provide different benefits from the pharmacological route, as it allows for more specificity by targeting specific precursors to neurogenesis and specific stem cell promoters. It also allows for temporal specificity with the interaction of certain drugs. This is beneficial in looking specifically at neurogenesis in adulthood, after normal development of other regions in the brain.
</p><p>The herpes simplex virus thymidine kinase (HSV-TK) has been used in studies in conjunction with antiviral drugs to inhibit adult neurogenesis. It works by targeting stem cells using glial fibrillary acidic proteins and nestin expression. These targeted stem cells undergo cell death instead of cell proliferation when exposed to antiviral drugs.
</p><p>Cre protein is also commonly used in targeting stem cells that will undergo gene changes upon treatment with tamoxifen.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Irradiation">Irradiation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Irradiation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Irradiation, the process of exposing parts of something or someone to radiation, is a method that allows for very specific inhibition of adult neurogenesis. It can be targeted to the brain to avoid affecting other systems and having nonspecific effects. It can even be used to target specific brain regions, which is important in determining how adult neurogenesis in different areas of the brain affects behavior.
</p><p>Irradiation has previously been tested in adult rats, with no significant changes in cognition having been reported. However, neurogenesis in this study was stopped from progressing when the irradiation was specifically directed towards the hippocampus.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>However, irradiation is more expensive than the other methods and also requires large equipment with trained individuals.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Inhibition_of_adult_neurogenesis_in_the_hippocampus">Inhibition of adult neurogenesis in the hippocampus</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Inhibition of adult neurogenesis in the hippocampus"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Many studies have observed how inhibiting adult neurogenesis in other mammals, such as rats and mice, affects their behavior.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Inhibition of adult neurogenesis in the hippocampus has been shown to have various effects on learning and memory, conditioning, and investigative behaviors.
</p><p>Impaired fear conditioning has been seen in studies involving rats with a lack of adult neurogenesis in the hippocampus.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Inhibition of adult neurogenesis in the hippocampus has also been linked to changes in behavior in tasks involving investigation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Rats also show decreased contextualized freezing behaviors in response to contextualized fear and impairment in learning spatial locations when lacking adult neurogenesis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Effects_on_pattern_separation">Effects on pattern separation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Effects on pattern separation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The changes in learning and memory seen in the studies mentioned previously are thought to be related to the role of adult neurogenesis in regulating pattern separation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Pattern separation is defined as "a process to remove redundancy from similar inputs so that events can be separated from each other and interference can be reduced, and in addition can produce a more orthogonal, sparse, and categorized set of outputs."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>This impairment in pattern separation could explain the impairments seen in other learning and memory tasks. A decreased ability in reducing interference could lead to greater difficulty in forming and retaining new memories,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> although it's hard to discriminate between effects of neurogenesis in learning and pattern separation due to limitations in the interpretation of behavioral results.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-França17&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-França17-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Studies show that rats with inhibited adult neurogenesis demonstrate difficulty in differentiating and learning contextualized fear conditioning.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Rats with blocked adult neurogenesis also show impaired differential freezing when they are required to differentiate between similar contexts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This also affects their spatial recognition in radial arm maze tests when the arms are closer together rather than when they are further apart.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A meta-analysis of behavioral studies evaluating the effect of neurogenesis in different pattern separation tests has shown a consistent effect of neurogenesis ablation on performance, although there are exceptions in the literature.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-França17&#95;63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-França17-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Effects_on_behavioral_inhibition">Effects on behavioral inhibition</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Effects on behavioral inhibition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Behavioral inhibition is important in rats and other animals in halting whatever they are currently doing in order to reassess a situation in response to a threat or anything else that may require their attention.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;13-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Rats with lesioned hippocampi show less behavioral inhibition when exposed to threats, such as cat odor.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The disruption of normal cell proliferation and development of the dentate gyrus in developing rats also impairs their freezing response, which is an example of behavior inhibition, when exposed to an unfamiliar adult male rat.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>This impairment in behavioral inhibition also ties into the process of learning and memory, as repressing wrong answers or behaviors requires the ability to inhibit that response.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;13-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Implications">Implications</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Implications"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Role_in_learning">Role in learning</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Role in learning"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The functional relevance of adult neurogenesis is uncertain,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but there is some evidence that hippocampal adult neurogenesis is important for <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-G.&#95;Neves&#95;2008&#95;65–75&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G._Neves_2008_65–75-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Multiple mechanisms for the relationship between increased neurogenesis and improved cognition have been suggested, including computational theories to demonstrate that new neurons increase memory capacity,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> reduce interference between memories,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or add information about time to memories.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Given that the rate of the neurogenesis does not change substantially during the adulthood, it has been proposed that unique <a href="/wiki/Episodic_memories" class="mw-redirect" title="Episodic memories">episodic memories</a> can be created by simply relying on the increased capacity of the young neurons of a particular age to establish stable new <a href="/wiki/Synapses" class="mw-redirect" title="Synapses">synapses</a> with peers representing the unique features of an event to be memorized<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Experiments aimed at ablating neurogenesis have proven inconclusive, but several studies have proposed neurogenic-dependence in some types of learning,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and others seeing no effect.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Studies have demonstrated that the act of learning itself is associated with increased neuronal survival.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the overall findings that adult neurogenesis is important for any kind of learning are equivocal.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Alzheimer's_disease"><span id="Alzheimer.27s_disease"></span>Alzheimer's disease</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Alzheimer&#039;s disease"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Some studies suggest that decreased hippocampal neurogenesis can lead to development of <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer&#39;s disease">Alzheimer's disease</a> (AD).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Donovan&#95;et&#95;al,&#95;2006&#95;77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donovan_et_al,_2006-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Yet, others hypothesize that AD patients have increased neurogenesis in the CA1 region of Ammon's horn (the principal region of AD hippocampal pathology) in order to compensate for neuronal loss.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jin&#95;et&#95;al,&#95;2003&#95;78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jin_et_al,_2003-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While the exact nature of the relationship between neurogenesis and Alzheimer's disease is unknown, <a href="/wiki/Insulin-like_growth_factor_1" title="Insulin-like growth factor 1">insulin-like growth factor 1</a>-stimulated neurogenesis produces major changes in hippocampal plasticity and seems to be involved in Alzheimer's pathology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Foster&#95;et&#95;al,&#95;2011&#95;79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foster_et_al,_2011-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Allopregnanolone" title="Allopregnanolone">Allopregnanolone</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Neurosteroid" title="Neurosteroid">neurosteroid</a>, aids the continued neurogenesis in the brain. Levels of allopregnanolone in the brain decline in <a href="/wiki/Old_age" title="Old age">old age</a> and Alzheimer's disease.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Allopregnanolone has been shown through reversing impairment of neurogenesis to reverse the <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_deficit" class="mw-redirect" title="Cognitive deficit">cognitive deficits</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Mouse_model" class="mw-redirect" title="Mouse model">mouse model</a> of Alzheimer's disease.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eph_receptor" class="mw-redirect" title="Eph receptor">Eph receptors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ephrin" title="Ephrin">ephrin</a> signaling have been shown to regulate adult neurogenesis in the <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">hippocampus</a> and have been studied as potential targets to treat some symptoms of AD.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Molecules associated with the pathology of AD, including <a href="/wiki/ApoE" class="mw-redirect" title="ApoE">ApoE</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photosystem_I" title="Photosystem I">PS1</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amyloid_precursor_protein" class="mw-redirect" title="Amyloid precursor protein">APP</a>, have also been found to impact adult neurogenesis in the hippocampus.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Schizophrenia">Schizophrenia</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Schizophrenia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Studies suggest that people with <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a> have a reduced hippocampus volume, which is believed to be caused by a reduction of adult neurogenesis.  Correspondingly, this phenomenon might be the underlying cause of many of the symptoms of the disease. Furthermore, several research papers referred to four genes, dystrobrevin binding protein 1 (DTNBP1), neuregulin 1 (NRG1), disrupted in schizophrenia 1 (DISC1), and neuregulin 1 receptor (ERBB4), as being possibly responsible for this deficit in the normal regeneration of neurons.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Similarities between depression and schizophrenia suggest a possible biological link between the two diseases. However, further research must be done in order to clearly demonstrate this relationship.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Stress_and_depression">Stress and depression</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Stress and depression"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Many now believe stress to be the most significant factor for the onset of <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a>. As discussed above, hippocampal cells are sensitive to stress which can lead to decreased neurogenesis. This area is being considered more frequently when examining the causes and treatments of depression. Studies have shown that removing the <a href="/wiki/Adrenal_gland" title="Adrenal gland">adrenal gland</a> in rats caused increased neurogenesis in the <a href="/wiki/Dentate_gyrus" title="Dentate gyrus">dentate gyrus</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jacobs,&#95;B.&#95;L.,&#95;H.&#95;van&#95;Praag,&#95;F.&#95;H.&#95;Gage&#95;2000&#95;87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs,_B._L.,_H._van_Praag,_F._H._Gage_2000-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The adrenal gland is responsible for producing <a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">cortisol</a> in response to a stressor, a substance that when produced in chronic amounts causes the down regulation of <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">serotonin</a> receptors and suppresses the birth of neurons.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It was shown in the same study that administration of corticosterone to normal animals suppressed neurogenesis, the opposite effect.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jacobs,&#95;B.&#95;L.,&#95;H.&#95;van&#95;Praag,&#95;F.&#95;H.&#95;Gage&#95;2000&#95;87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs,_B._L.,_H._van_Praag,_F._H._Gage_2000-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The most typical class of antidepressants administered for this disease are <a href="/wiki/Selective_serotonin_reuptake_inhibitors" class="mw-redirect" title="Selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors">selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors</a> (SSRIs)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and their efficacy may be explained by neurogenesis. In a normal brain, an increase in serotonin causes suppression of the <a href="/wiki/Corticotropin-releasing_hormone" title="Corticotropin-releasing hormone">corticotropin-releasing hormone</a> (CRH) through connection to the hippocampus. It directly acts on the <a href="/wiki/Paraventricular_nucleus" title="Paraventricular nucleus">paraventricular nucleus</a> to decrease CRH release and down regulate norepinephrine functioning in the <a href="/wiki/Locus_coeruleus" title="Locus coeruleus">locus coeruleus</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jacobs,&#95;B.&#95;L.,&#95;H.&#95;van&#95;Praag,&#95;F.&#95;H.&#95;Gage&#95;2000&#95;87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs,_B._L.,_H._van_Praag,_F._H._Gage_2000-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Because CRH is being repressed, the decrease in neurogenesis that is associated with elevated levels of it is also being reversed. This allows for the production of more brain cells, in particular at the 5-HT1a receptor in the dentate gyrus of the hippocampus which has been shown to improve symptoms of depression. It normally takes neurons approximately three to six weeks to mature,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which is approximately the same amount of time it takes for SSRIs to take effect. This correlation strengthens the hypothesis that SSRIs act through neurogenesis to decrease the symptoms of depression.  Some neuroscientists have expressed skepticism that neurogenesis is functionally significant, given that a tiny number of nascent neurons are actually integrated into existing neural circuitry. However, a recent study used the irradiation of nascent hippocampal neurons in non-human primates (NHP) to demonstrate that neurogenesis is required for antidepressant efficacy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Adult-born neurons appear to have a role in the regulation of <a href="/wiki/Stress_(psychological)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stress (psychological)">stress</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-schl1&#95;92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schl1-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-surg1&#95;93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-surg1-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Studies have linked neurogenesis to the beneficial actions of specific <a href="/wiki/Antidepressant" title="Antidepressant">antidepressants</a>, suggesting a connection between decreased hippocampal neurogenesis and depression.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In a pioneer study, scientists demonstrated that the behavioral benefits of antidepressant administration in <a href="/wiki/Mice" class="mw-redirect" title="Mice">mice</a> is reversed when neurogenesis is prevented with <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">x-irradiation</a> techniques.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In fact, newborn neurons are more excitable than older neurons due to a differential expression of <a href="/wiki/Gamma-Aminobutyric_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamma-Aminobutyric acid">GABA</a> receptors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A plausible model, therefore, is that these neurons augment the role of the hippocampus in the negative feedback mechanism of the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal_axis" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis">HPA-axis</a> (physiological stress) and perhaps in inhibiting the <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">amygdala</a> (the region of brain responsible for fearful responses to stimuli).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (September 2009)">vague</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Indeed, suppression of adult neurogenesis can lead to an increased HPA-axis stress response in mildly stressful situations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-schl1&#95;92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schl1-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is consistent with numerous findings linking stress-relieving activities (learning, exposure to a new yet benign environment, and exercise) to increased levels of neurogenesis, as well as the observation that animals exposed to physiological stress (cortisol) or <a href="/wiki/Psychological_stress" title="Psychological stress">psychological stress</a> (e.g. isolation) show markedly decreased levels of newborn neurons. Under chronic stress conditions, the elevation of newborn neurons by antidepressants improves the hippocampal-dependent control on the stress response; without newborn neurons, antidepressants are unable to restore the regulation of the stress response and recovery becomes impossible.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-surg1&#95;93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-surg1-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some studies have hypothesized that learning and memory are linked to depression, and that neurogenesis may promote <a href="/wiki/Neuroplasticity" title="Neuroplasticity">neuroplasticity</a>. One study proposes that mood may be regulated, at a base level, by plasticity, and thus <i>not chemistry</i>. Accordingly, the effects of antidepressant treatment would only be secondary to change in plasticity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However another study has demonstrated an interaction between antidepressants and plasticity; the antidepressant fluoxetine has been shown to restore plasticity in the adult rat brain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The results of this study imply that instead of being secondary to changes in plasticity, antidepressant therapy could promote it.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Major_depressive_disorder">Major depressive disorder</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Major depressive disorder"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Research indicates that adult hippocampal neurogenesis is inversely related to major depressive disorder (MDD).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Neurogenesis is decreased in the hippocampus of animal models of major depressive disorder, and many treatments for the disorder, including <a href="/wiki/Antidepressant" title="Antidepressant">antidepressant</a> medication and <a href="/wiki/Electroconvulsive_therapy" title="Electroconvulsive therapy">electroconvulsive therapy</a>, increase hippocampal neurogenesis. It has been theorized that decreased hippocampal neurogenesis in individuals with major depressive disorder may be related to the high levels of stress hormones called <a href="/wiki/Glucocorticoid" title="Glucocorticoid">glucocorticoids</a>, which are also associated with the disorder. The hippocampus instructs the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal_axis" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis">hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis</a> to produce fewer glucocorticoids when glucocorticoid levels are high. A malfunctioning hippocampus, therefore, might explain the chronically high glucocorticoid levels in individuals with major depressive disorder. However, some studies have indicated that hippocampal neurogenesis is not lower in individuals with major depressive disorder and that blood glucocorticoid levels do not change when hippocampal neurogenesis changes, so the associations are still uncertain.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Parkinson's_disease"><span id="Parkinson.27s_disease"></span>Parkinson's disease</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Parkinson&#039;s disease"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson&#39;s disease">Parkinson's disease</a> is a neurodegenerative disorder characterized by a progressive loss of <a href="/wiki/Dopaminergic_neuron" class="mw-redirect" title="Dopaminergic neuron">dopaminergic neurons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Substantia_nigra" title="Substantia nigra">substantia nigra</a>. Transplantation of fetal dopaminergic <a href="/wiki/Precursor_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Precursor cells">precursor cells</a> has paved the way for the possibility of a cell replacement therapy that could ameliorate clinical symptoms in affected
patients.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Arias07&#95;101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arias07-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In recent years, scientists have provided evidence for the existence of neural stem cells with the potential to produce new neurons, particularly of a dopaminergic phenotype, in the adult mammalian brain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Experimental depletion of dopamine in rodents decreases precursor cell proliferation in both the subependymal zone and the subgranular zone.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hogl&#95;105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hogl-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Proliferation is restored completely by a selective agonist of D2-like (D2L) receptors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hogl&#95;105-1" class="reference"><a
