href="#cite_note-Hogl-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Neural stem cells have been identified in the neurogenic brain regions, where neurogenesis is constitutively ongoing, but also in the non-neurogenic zones, such as the midbrain and the striatum, where neurogenesis is not thought to occur under normal physiological conditions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Arias07&#95;101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arias07-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Newer research has shown that there in fact is neurogenesis in the striatum.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
A detailed understanding of the factors governing adult neural stem cells <i>in vivo</i> may ultimately lead to elegant cell therapies for neurodegenerative disorders such as Parkinson's disease by mobilizing autologous endogenous neural stem cells to replace degenerated neurons.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Arias07&#95;101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arias07-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Traumatic_brain_injury">Traumatic brain injury</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Traumatic brain injury"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Traumatic_brain_injury" title="Traumatic brain injury">Traumatic brain injuries</a> vary in their mechanism of injury, producing a blunt or penetrating trauma resulting in a <a href="/wiki/Primary_and_secondary_brain_injury" title="Primary and secondary brain injury">primary and secondary injury</a> with <a href="/wiki/Excitotoxicity" title="Excitotoxicity">excitotoxicity</a> and relatively wide spread neuronal death. Due to the overwhelming number of traumatic brain injuries as a result of the <a href="/wiki/War_on_terror" title="War on terror">war on terror</a>, tremendous amounts of research have been placed towards a better understanding of the pathophysiology of traumatic brain injuries as well as <a href="/wiki/Neuroprotection" title="Neuroprotection">neuroprotective</a> interventions and possible interventions prompting restorative neurogenesis. Hormonal interventions, such as progesterone, estrogen, and allopregnanolone have been examined heavily in recent decades as possible neuroprotective agents following traumatic brain injuries to reduce the inflammation response stunt neuronal death.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In rodents, lacking the regenerative capacity for adult neurogenesis, the activation of stem cells following administration of α7 nicotinic acetylcholine receptor agonist, <a href="/wiki/PNU-282,987" title="PNU-282,987">PNU-282987</a>, has been identified in damaged retinas with follow-up work examining activation of neurogenesis in mammals after traumatic brain injury.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Currently, there is no medical intervention that has passed <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_clinical_research" title="Phases of clinical research">phase-III</a> clinical trials for use in the human population.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Effects_of_sleep_reduction">Effects of sleep reduction</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Effects of sleep reduction"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>One study has linked lack of sleep to a reduction in rodent hippocampal neurogenesis. The proposed mechanism for the observed decrease was increased levels of <a href="/wiki/Glucocorticoids" class="mw-redirect" title="Glucocorticoids">glucocorticoids</a>.  It was shown that two weeks of <a href="/wiki/Sleep_deprivation" title="Sleep deprivation">sleep deprivation</a> acted as a neurogenesis-inhibitor, which was reversed after return of normal sleep and even shifted to a temporary increase in normal cell proliferation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mirescu06&#95;112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mirescu06-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> More precisely, when levels of corticosterone are elevated, sleep deprivation inhibits this process. Nonetheless, normal levels of neurogenesis after chronic sleep deprivation return after 2 weeks, with a temporary increase of neurogenesis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mirescu06&#95;112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mirescu06-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While this is recognized, overlooked is the blood glucose demand exhibited during temporary diabetic hypoglycemic states.  The American Diabetes Association amongst many documents the pseudosenilia and agitation found during temporary hypoglycemic states.  Much more clinical documentation is needed to competently demonstrate the link between decreased hematologic glucose and neuronal activity and mood.
</p><p>On the other hand, short-term sleep deprivation is known to have an antidepressant effect in humans. In animal models, it increases adult neurogenesis and reduces depression-like behavior.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Factors_affecting">Factors affecting</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Factors affecting"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Changes_in_old_age">Changes in old age</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Changes in old age"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Neurogenesis is substantially reduced in the hippocampus of aged animals, raising the possibility that it may be linked to age-related declines in hippocampal function. For example, the rate of neurogenesis in aged animals is predictive of memory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, new born cells in aged animals are functionally integrated.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Given that neurogenesis occurs throughout life, it might be expected that the hippocampus would steadily increase in size during adulthood, and that therefore the number of granule cells would be increased in aged animals. However, this is not the case, indicating that proliferation is balanced by cell death. Thus, it is not the addition of new neurons into the hippocampus that seems to be linked to hippocampal functions, but rather the rate of turnover of granule cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Effects_of_exercise">Effects of exercise</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Effects of exercise"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neurobiological_effects_of_physical_exercise#Neuroplasticity" title="Neurobiological effects of physical exercise">Neurobiological effects of physical exercise §&#160;Neuroplasticity</a></div>
<p>Scientists have shown that physical activity in the form of voluntary exercise results in an increase in the number of newborn neurons in the hippocampus of mice and rats.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Praag99&#95;117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Praag99-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  These and other studies have shown that learning in both species can be enhanced by physical exercise.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Praag99&#95;117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Praag99-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Recent research has shown that <a href="/wiki/Brain-derived_neurotrophic_factor" title="Brain-derived neurotrophic factor">brain-derived neurotrophic factor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insulin-like_growth_factor_1" title="Insulin-like growth factor 1">insulin-like growth factor 1</a> are key mediators of exercise-induced neurogenesis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Trejo&#95;et&#95;al,&#95;2001&#95;119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trejo_et_al,_2001-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Exercise increases the production of BDNF, as well as the NR2B subunit of the NMDA receptor.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;118-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Exercise increases the uptake of IGF-1 from the bloodstream into various brain regions, including the hippocampus. In addition, IGF-1 alters c-fos expression in the hippocampus. When IGF-1 is blocked, exercise no longer induces neurogenesis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Trejo&#95;et&#95;al,&#95;2001&#95;119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trejo_et_al,_2001-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other research demonstrated that exercising mice that did not produce beta-endorphin, a mood-elevating hormone, had no change in neurogenesis. Yet, mice that did produce this hormone, along with exercise, exhibited an increase in newborn cells and their rate of survival.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
While the association between exercise-mediated neurogenesis and enhancement of learning remains unclear, this study could have strong implications in the fields of aging and/or <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer&#39;s disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Effects_of_cannabinoids">Effects of cannabinoids</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Effects of cannabinoids"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Star_of_life.svg/60px-Star_of_life.svg.png" decoding="async" width="45" height="44" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Star_of_life.svg/120px-Star_of_life.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="198" data-file-height="192" /></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs more <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)">reliable medical references</a> for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a> or relies too heavily on <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Primary_sources" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Primary sources">primary sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please review the contents of the section and <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit">add the appropriate references</a> if you can. Unsourced or poorly sourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>. <small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Adult+neurogenesis%22">"Adult neurogenesis"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Adult+neurogenesis%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Adult+neurogenesis%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Adult+neurogenesis%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Adult+neurogenesis%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Adult+neurogenesis%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2016</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Some studies have shown that the stimulation of the <a href="/wiki/Cannabinoid" title="Cannabinoid">cannabinoids</a> results in the growth of new nerve cells in the hippocampus from both embryonic and adult stem cells. In 2005 a clinical study of rats at the University of Saskatchewan showed regeneration of nerve cells in the hippocampus.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jiang05&#95;121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jiang05-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Studies have shown that a synthetic drug resembling <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrocanabinol" class="mw-redirect" title="Tetrahydrocanabinol">THC</a>, the main psychoactive ingredient in marijuana, provides some protection against <a href="/wiki/Brain_inflammation" class="mw-redirect" title="Brain inflammation">brain inflammation</a>, which might result in better memory at an older age. This is due to receptors in the system that can also influence the production of new neurons.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Nonetheless, a study directed at Rutgers University demonstrated how synchronization of action potentials in the hippocampus of rats was altered after THC administration. Lack of synchronization corresponded with impaired performance in a standard test of memory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Recent studies indicate that a natural cannabinoid of cannabis, cannabidiol (CBD), increases adult neurogenesis while having no effect on learning. THC however impaired learning and had no effect on neurogenesis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
A greater CBD to THC ratio in hair analyses of cannabis users correlates with protection against gray matter reduction in the right hippocampus.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> CBD has also been observed to attenuate the deficits in prose recall and visuo-spatial associative memory of those currently under the influence of cannabis,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> implying neuroprotective effects against heavy THC exposure. Neurogenesis might play a role in its neuroprotective effects, but further research is required.
</p><p>A few studies have reported a positive association between THC and hippocampal neurogenesis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Biphasic&#95;effects&#95;of&#95;THC&#95;in&#95;memory&#95;a&#95;128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biphasic_effects_of_THC_in_memory_a-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some of them hypotethize a biphasic effect,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Biphasic&#95;effects&#95;of&#95;THC&#95;in&#95;memory&#95;a&#95;128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biphasic_effects_of_THC_in_memory_a-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> some of them express that part of the negative effects could be attributable to neuroadaptation due to exposure at a specific period of life, and that it could be reversed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Regulation">Regulation</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Regulation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Summary_of_the_signalling_pathways_in_the_neural_stem_cell_microenvironment.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Summary_of_the_signalling_pathways_in_the_neural_stem_cell_microenvironment.jpg/250px-Summary_of_the_signalling_pathways_in_the_neural_stem_cell_microenvironment.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="243" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5c/Summary_of_the_signalling_pathways_in_the_neural_stem_cell_microenvironment.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="486" /></a><figcaption>Summary of the <a href="/wiki/Signalling_pathway" class="mw-redirect" title="Signalling pathway">signalling pathways</a> in the neural stem cell microenvironment.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Many factors may affect the rate of hippocampal neurogenesis. <a href="/wiki/Exercise" title="Exercise">Exercise</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Environmental_enrichment_(neural)" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental enrichment (neural)">enriched environment</a> have been shown to promote the survival of neurons and the successful integration of newborn cells into the existing hippocampus.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid15766532&#95;131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15766532-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another factor is <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> injury since neurogenesis occurs after <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_ischemia" class="mw-redirect" title="Cerebral ischemia">cerebral ischemia</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Epileptic_seizure" class="mw-redirect" title="Epileptic seizure">epileptic seizures</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_meningitis" class="mw-redirect" title="Bacterial meningitis">bacterial meningitis</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On the other hand, conditions such as <a href="/wiki/Stress_(medicine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stress (medicine)">chronic stress</a>, viral infection and <a href="/wiki/Aging" class="mw-redirect" title="Aging">aging</a> can result in a decreased neuronal proliferation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jiang05&#95;121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jiang05-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Circulating factors within the blood may reduce neurogenesis. In healthy aging humans, the plasma and cerebrospinal fluid levels of certain chemokines are elevated. In a mouse model, plasma levels of these chemokines correlate with reduced neurogenesis, suggesting that neurogenesis may be modulated by certain global age-dependent systemic changes. These chemokines include <a href="/wiki/CCL11" title="CCL11">CCL11</a>, <a href="/wiki/CCL2" title="CCL2">CCL2</a> and <a href="/wiki/CCL12" title="CCL12">CCL12</a>, which are highly localized on mouse and human chromosomes, implicating a genetic locus in aging.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-G.&#95;Neves&#95;2008&#95;65–75&#95;69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G._Neves_2008_65–75-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another study implicated the cytokine, IL-1beta, which is produced by glia. That study found that blocking IL-1 could partially prevent the severe impairment of neurogenesis caused by a viral infection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Epigenetic_Regulation_of_Neurogenesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Epigenetic Regulation of Neurogenesis">Epigenetic regulation</a> also plays a large role in neurogenesis. DNA methylation is critical in the fate-determination of adult neural stem cells in the <a href="/wiki/Subventricular_zone" title="Subventricular zone">subventricular zone</a> for post-natal neurogenesis through the regulation of neuronic genes such as <i>Dlx2</i>, <i>Neurog2</i>, and <i>Sp8</i>. Many <a href="/wiki/MicroRNA" title="MicroRNA">microRNAs</a> such as miR-124 and miR-9 have been shown to influence cortical size and layering during development.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ephrin_A2" title="Ephrin A2">ephrin-A2</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ephrin_A3" title="Ephrin A3">ephrin-A3</a> have been shown to negatively regulate adult neurogenesis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adult_neurogenesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Early neuroanatomists, including <a href="/wiki/Santiago_Ram%C3%B3n_y_Cajal" title="Santiago Ramón y Cajal">Santiago Ramón y Cajal</a>, considered the nervous system fixed and incapable of regeneration.  The first evidence of adult mammalian neurogenesis in the <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">cerebral cortex</a> was presented by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Altman" title="Joseph Altman">Joseph Altman</a> in 1962,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Altman62&#95;51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altman62-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> followed by a demonstration of adult neurogenesis in the dentate gyrus of the hippocampus in 1963.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  In 1969, Joseph Altman discovered and named the <a href="/wiki/Rostral_migratory_stream" title="Rostral migratory stream">rostral migratory stream</a> as the source of adult generated granule cell neurons in the olfactory bulb.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Up until the 1980s, the scientific community ignored these findings despite use of the most direct method of demonstrating cell proliferation in the early studies, i.e. 3H-thymidine autoradiography.  By that time, Shirley Bayer<sup id="cite&#95;ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Kaplan_(biologist)" title="Michael Kaplan (biologist)">Michael Kaplan</a>) again showed that adult neurogenesis exists in mammals (rats), and Nottebohm showed the same phenomenon in birds<sup id="cite&#95;ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> sparking renewed interest in the topic.  Studies in the 1990s<sup id="cite&#95;ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> finally put research on adult neurogenesis into a mainstream pursuit.  Also in the early 1990s hippocampal neurogenesis was demonstrated in non-human primates and humans.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  More recently, neurogenesis in the cerebellum of adult rabbits has also been characterized.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>153<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Further, some authors (particularly <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Gould_(psychologist)" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth Gould (psychologist)">Elizabeth Gould</a>) have suggested that adult neurogenesis may also occur in regions within the brain not generally associated with neurogenesis including the <a href="/wiki/Neopallium" class="mw-redirect" title="Neopallium">neocortex</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>155<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, others<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid11826088&#95;157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11826088-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> have questioned the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific evidence</a> of these findings, arguing that the new <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> may be of <a href="/wiki/Glia" title="Glia">glial origin</a>. Recent research has elucidated the regulatory effect of <a href="/wiki/GABA" title="GABA">GABA</a> on neural stem cells. GABA's well-known inhibitory effects across the brain also affect the local circuitry that triggers a stem cell to become dormant. They found that <a href="/wiki/Diazepam" title="Diazepam">diazepam</a> (Valium) has a similar effect.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>158<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
